<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Climate change mitigation</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="660" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg/220px-Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg/330px-Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg/440px-Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Observed_CO2_Emissions_from_fossil_fuel_burning_vs_IPCC_scenarios.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fossil fuel related CO<sub>2</sub> emissions compared to five of IPCC's emissions scenarios. The dips are related to global recessions. Data from <a class="external text" href="http://www.ipcc.ch/ipccreports/sres/emission/data/allscen.xls" rel="nofollow">IPCC SRES scenarios</a>; <a class="external text" href="http://www.iea.org/co2highlights/co2Highlights.XLS" rel="nofollow">Data spreadsheet included with International Energy Agency's "CO2 Emissions from Fuel Combustion 2010 – Highlights"</a>; and <a class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2011/may/29/carbon-emissions-nuclearpower" rel="nofollow">Supplemental IEA data</a>. Image source: <a href="/wiki/Skeptical_Science" title="Skeptical Science">Skeptical Science</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="835" data-file-width="951" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/220px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/330px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg/440px-Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_Anomaly.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global mean surface temperature change from 1880 to 2016, relative to the 1951–1980 mean. The black line is the global annual mean and the red line is the five-year <a href="/wiki/Local_regression" title="Local regression">lowess smooth</a>. The blue <a href="/wiki/Error_bar" title="Error bar">uncertainty bars</a> show a 95% confidence limit. Source: <a class="external text" href="http://data.giss.nasa.gov/gistemp/" rel="nofollow">NASA GISS</a>. <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">Global dimming</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_aerosol" title="Sulfate aerosol">sulfate aerosol</a> air pollution, between 1950 and 1980 is believed to have mitigated global warming somewhat.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/220px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/330px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/440px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global carbon dioxide emissions from human activities 1800–2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="650" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg/220px-Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg/330px-Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg/440px-Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_by_sector_2000.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas</a> emissions by sector. See <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060513035236/http://cait.wri.org/figures.php?page=World-FlowChart&amp;view=100" rel="nofollow">World Resources Institute</a> for a detailed breakdown.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="1030" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/220px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/330px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/440px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global public support for energy sources, based on a survey by Ipsos (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Climate change mitigation</b> consists of actions to limit the magnitude or rate of long-term <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Climate change mitigation generally involves reductions in human (<a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a>) emissions of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> (GHGs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_mitigation_options_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_mitigation_options-4">[4]</a></sup> Mitigation may also be achieved by increasing the capacity of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sinks</a>, e.g., through <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_mitigation_options_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_mitigation_options-4">[4]</a></sup> Mitigation policies can substantially reduce the risks associated with human-induced global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="International Panel on Climate Change">IPCC's</a> 2014 <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">assessment report</a>, "Mitigation is a public good; climate change is a case of the '<a href="/wiki/Tragedy_of_the_commons" title="Tragedy of the commons">tragedy of the commons</a>'. Effective climate change mitigation will not be achieved if each agent (individual, institution or country) acts independently in its own selfish interest (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_cooperation" title="International cooperation">International cooperation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">Emissions trading</a>), suggesting the need for collective action. Some <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation" title="Climate change adaptation">adaptation</a> actions, on the other hand, have characteristics of a private good as benefits of actions may accrue more directly to the individuals, regions, or countries that undertake them, at least in the short term. Nevertheless, financing such adaptive activities remains an issue, particularly for poor individuals and countries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR5-WG3-Chap3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-AR5-WG3-Chap3-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of mitigation include reducing energy demand by increasing <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">phasing out fossil fuels</a> by switching to <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon energy</a> sources, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">removing carbon dioxide</a> from Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_mitigation_options_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_mitigation_options-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> for example, through improved <a href="/wiki/Building_insulation" title="Building insulation">building insulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Another approach to climate change mitigation is <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most countries are parties to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The ultimate objective of the UNFCCC is to stabilize atmospheric concentrations of GHGs at a level that would prevent dangerous human interference of the climate system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Scientific analysis can provide information on the impacts of climate change, but deciding which impacts are dangerous requires value judgments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wg_2_ch19_dangerous_climate_change_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-wg_2_ch19_dangerous_climate_change-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, Parties to the UNFCCC agreed that future global warming should be limited to below 2.0 °C (3.6 °F) relative to the pre-<a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial</a> level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> of 2015 this was confirmed, but was revised with a new target laying down "parties will do the best" to achieve warming below 1.5 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-14">[14]</a></sup> The current trajectory of global greenhouse gas emissions does not appear to be consistent with limiting global warming to below 1.5 or 2 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Other mitigation policies have been proposed, some of which are more stringent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-existing_policies_inadequate_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-existing_policies_inadequate-16">[16]</a></sup> or modest<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_vuuren_mitigation_policy_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_vuuren_mitigation_policy-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nordhaus_2010_cba_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nordhaus_2010_cba-18">[18]</a></sup> than the 2 °C limit.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_concentrations_and_stabilization">Greenhouse gas concentrations and stabilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas concentrations and stabilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Removal_from_the_atmosphere_and_global_warming_potential" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas § Removal from the atmosphere and global warming potential</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:324px;max-width:324px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="1436" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png/320px-Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png/480px-Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png/640px-Stabilizing_carbon_dioxide_emissions_at_their_present_level_will_not_stabilize_its_concentration_in_the_atmosphere.png 2x" width="320"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Stabilizing CO<sub>2</sub> emissions at their present level would not stabilize its concentration in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:322px;max-width:322px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="1436" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png/320px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png/480px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png/640px-Stabilizing_the_atmospheric_concentration_of_carbon_dioxide_at_a_constant_level_would_require_emissions_to_be_effectively_eliminated.png 2x" width="320"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Stabilizing the atmospheric concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> at a constant level would require emissions to be effectively eliminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>One of the issues often discussed in relation to climate change mitigation is the stabilization of greenhouse gas concentrations in the atmosphere. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) has the ultimate objective of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avoiding_Dangerous_Climate_Change" title="Avoiding Dangerous Climate Change">preventing "dangerous" anthropogenic (i.e., human) interference of the climate system</a>. As is stated in Article 2 of the Convention, this requires that greenhouse gas (GHG) concentrations are stabilized in the atmosphere at a level where <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">ecosystems</a> can adapt naturally to climate change, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">food production</a> is not threatened, and <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_global_warming" title="Economics of global warming">economic development</a> can proceed in a sustainable fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a number of anthropogenic greenhouse gases. These include <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a>: CO<sub>2</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>), and a group of gases referred to as <a href="/wiki/Halocarbon" title="Halocarbon">halocarbons</a>.  Another greenhouse gas, <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Role_of_water_vapor" title="Greenhouse gas">water vapor</a>, has also risen as an indirect result of human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The emissions reductions necessary to stabilize the atmospheric concentrations of these gases varies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> is the most important of the anthropogenic greenhouse gases (see <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a difference between stabilizing CO<sub>2</sub> emissions and stabilizing atmospheric concentrations of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Stabilizing emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> at current levels would not lead to a stabilization in the atmospheric concentration of CO<sub>2</sub>. In fact, stabilizing emissions at current levels would result in the atmospheric concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> continuing to rise over the 21st century and beyond (see the graphs opposite).
</p><p>The reason for this is that human activities are adding CO<sub>2</sub> to the atmosphere faster than natural processes can remove it (see <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere</a> for a complete explanation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup> This is analogous to a flow of water into a bathtub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> So long as the tap runs water (analogous to the emission of carbon dioxide) into the tub faster than water escapes through the plughole (the natural removal of carbon dioxide from the atmosphere), then the level of water in the tub (analogous to the concentration of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere) will continue to rise.
</p><p>According to some studies, stabilizing atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations would require anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions to be reduced by 80% relative to the peak emissions level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_co2_stabilization_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_co2_stabilization-25">[25]</a></sup> An 80% reduction in emissions would stabilize CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations for around a century, but even greater reductions would be required beyond this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_co2_stabilization_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_co2_stabilization-25">[25]</a></sup> Other research has found that, after leaving room for emissions for food production for 9 billion people and to keep the global temperature rise below 2 °C, emissions from energy production and transport will have to peak almost immediately in the developed world and decline at ca. 10% per annum until zero emissions are reached around 2030. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> energy and transport emissions would have to peak by 2025 and then decline similarly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Stabilizing the atmospheric concentration of the other greenhouse gasses humans emit also depends on how fast their emissions are added to the atmosphere, and how fast the GHGs are removed. Stabilization for these gases is described in the later section on <a href="#Non-CO2_greenhouse_gases">non-CO<sub>2</sub> GHGs</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 2018 an international team of scientist published research saying that the current mitigation policy in <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> is insufficient to limit the temperature rise to 2 degrees. They say that even if all the current pledges will be accomplished there is a chance for a 4.5 degree temperature rise in decades.  To preventing that, restoration of natural <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">Carbon sinks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">Carbon dioxide removal</a>, changes in society and values will be necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trajectories_of_the_Earth_System_in_the_Anthropocene_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trajectories_of_the_Earth_System_in_the_Anthropocene-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Projections</dt></dl>
<p>Projections of future greenhouse gas emissions are highly uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In the absence of policies to mitigate climate change, GHG emissions could rise significantly over the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_article_2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_article_2-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous assessments have considered how atmospheric GHG concentrations could be stabilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The lower the desired stabilization level, the sooner global GHG emissions must peak and decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> GHG concentrations are unlikely to stabilize this century without major policy changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_article_2_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_article_2-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1621" data-file-width="1463" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png/320px-Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png/480px-Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png/640px-Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_carbon_dioxide_emissions_and_atmospheric_concentrations_over_the_21st_century_for_reference_and_mitigation_scenarios.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projected carbon dioxide emissions and atmospheric concentrations over the 21st century for reference and mitigation scenarios</div></div></div>
</td>
<td><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:335px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="750" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/333px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/500px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/666px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 2x" width="333"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rate of world energy usage per day, from 1970 to 2010. Every fossil fuel source has increased in large amounts between 1970 and 2010, dominating all other energy sources. Hydroelectricity has increased at a slow steady rate over this same period, nuclear entered a period of rapid growth between 1970 and 1990 before leveling off. Other renewables, between 2000 and 2010 have, having started from a low usage rate, began to enter into a period of rapid growth. 1000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TWh" title="TWh">TWh</a>=1 PWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BP-Report-2012_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Report-2012-35">[35]</a></sup></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_consumption_by_power_source">Energy consumption by power source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Energy consumption by power source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="862" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG/220px-Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG/330px-Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG/440px-Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_Emissions_from_Electricity_Production_IEA.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Hydropower-Internalised Costs and Externalised Benefits"; Frans H. Koch; <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA)-Implementing Agreement for Hydropower Technologies and Programmes; 2000.</div></div></div>
<p>To create lasting climate change mitigation, the replacement of high carbon <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> power sources, such as conventional <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>—<a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>—with <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> sources is required. Fossil fuels supply humanity with the vast majority of our energy demands, and at a growing rate. In 2012 the IEA noted that coal accounted for half the increased energy use of the prior decade, growing faster than all renewable energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> together provide the majority of the generated <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> fraction of global total power consumption.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_and_means">Methods and means</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Methods and means">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">Emission intensity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source,_over_the_21st_century,_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1508" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png/220px-Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png/330px-Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png/440px-Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source%2C_over_the_21st_century%2C_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source,_over_the_21st_century,_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This graph shows the projected contribution of various energy sources to world primary electricity consumption (PEC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It is based on a climate change mitigation scenario, in which GHG emissions are substantially reduced over the 21st century. In the scenario, emission reductions are achieved using a portfolio of energy sources, as well as reductions in energy demand. <i>Also available in <a href="/wiki/File:Projected_global_primary_electricity_consumption_by_source,_over_the_21st_century,_for_a_climate_change_mitigation_scenario_(greyscale).png" title="File:Projected global primary electricity consumption by source, over the 21st century, for a climate change mitigation scenario (greyscale).png">greyscale</a>.</i></div></div></div>
<p>Assessments often suggest that GHG emissions can be reduced using a portfolio of low-carbon technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> At the core of most proposals is the reduction of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> (GHG) emissions through reducing energy waste and switching to <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a> sources of energy. As the cost of reducing GHG emissions in the <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> sector appears to be lower than in other sectors, such as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a> sector, the electricity sector may deliver the largest proportional carbon reductions under an economically efficient climate policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-issues_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-issues-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>"Economic tools can be useful in designing climate change mitigation policies." "While the limitations of economics and social welfare analysis, including cost–benefit analysis, are widely documented, economics nevertheless provides useful tools for assessing the pros and cons of taking, or not taking, action on climate change mitigation, as well as of adaptation measures, in achieving competing societal goals. Understanding these pros and cons can help in making policy decisions on climate change mitigation and can influence the actions taken by countries, institutions and individuals."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AR5-WG3-Chap3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-AR5-WG3-Chap3-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other frequently discussed means include efficiency, public transport, increasing <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy in automobiles</a> (which includes the use of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">electric hybrids</a>), charging <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a> by <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon electricity</a>, making <a href="/wiki/Individual_and_political_action_on_climate_change" title="Individual and political action on climate change">individual changes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Business_action_on_climate_change" title="Business action on climate change">changing business practices</a>. Many fossil fuel driven vehicles can be converted to use electricity, the US has the potential to supply electricity for 73% of light duty vehicles (LDV), using overnight charging. The US average CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for a battery-electric car is 180 grams per mile vs 430 grams per mile for a gasoline car.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The emissions would be displaced away from street level, where they have "high human-health implications. Increased use of electricity "generation for meeting the future transportation load is primarily fossil-fuel based", mostly natural gas, followed by coal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> but could also be met through nuclear, tidal, hydroelectric and other sources.
</p><p>A range of energy technologies may contribute to climate change mitigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomass_preprocessing" title="Biomass preprocessing">biomass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_energy" title="Ocean energy">ocean energy</a>, and; the use of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sinks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_W._Pacala" title="Stephen W. Pacala">Pacala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Socolow" title="Robert H. Socolow">Socolow</a> of Princeton<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pacala2004sws_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-pacala2004sws-46">[46]</a></sup> have proposed a 15 part program to reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by 1 billion metric tons per year − or 25 billion tons over the 50-year period using today's technologies as a type of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_game" title="Global warming game">global warming game</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another consideration is how future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socioeconomic_development" title="Socioeconomic development">socioeconomic development</a> proceeds. Development choices (or "pathways") can lead differences in GHG emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Political and social attitudes may affect how easy or difficult it is to implement effective policies to reduce emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_side_management">Demand side management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Demand side management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle_and_behavior">Lifestyle and behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Lifestyle and behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">Fifth Assessment Report</a> emphasises that behaviour, lifestyle, and cultural change have a high mitigation potential in some sectors, particularly when complementing technological and structural change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edenhofer-etal-2014_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-edenhofer-etal-2014-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20</span></sup>
In general, higher consumption lifestyles have a greater environmental impact. Several scientific studies have shown that when people, especially those living in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a> but more generally including all countries, wish to reduce their carbon footprint, there are four key "high-impact" actions they can take:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wynes-Nicholas-2017_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wynes-Nicholas-2017-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PimmJenkins_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-PimmJenkins-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>1. <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">Not having</a> an additional child (58.6 tonnes CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent emission reductions per year)</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>2. Living <a href="/wiki/Car-free_movement" title="Car-free movement">car-free</a> (2.4 tonnes CO<sub>2</sub>)</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>3. Avoiding one round-trip transatlantic flight (1.6 tonnes)</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>4. Eating a <a href="/wiki/Plant-based_diet" title="Plant-based diet">plant-based diet</a> (0.8 tonnes)</dd></dl>
<p>These appear to differ significantly from the popular advice for “greening” one's lifestyle, which seem to fall mostly into the “low-impact” category: Replacing a typical car with a hybrid (0.52 tonnes); Washing clothes in cold water (0.25 tonnes); Recycling (0.21 tonnes); Upgrading light bulbs (0.10 tonnes); etc. The researchers found that public discourse on reducing one's carbon footprint overwhelmingly focuses on low-impact behaviors, and that mention of the high-impact behaviors is almost non-existent in the mainstream media, government publications, K-12 school textbooks, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wynes-Nicholas-2017_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wynes-Nicholas-2017-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PimmJenkins_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-PimmJenkins-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The researchers added that “Our recommended high-impact actions are more effective than many more commonly discussed options (e.g. eating a plant-based diet saves eight times more emissions than upgrading light bulbs). More significantly, a US family who chooses to have one fewer child would provide the same level of emissions reductions as 684 teenagers who choose to adopt comprehensive recycling for the rest of their lives.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wynes-Nicholas-2017_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wynes-Nicholas-2017-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos-Ehrlich-2017-05-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PimmJenkins_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-PimmJenkins-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_change">Dietary change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dietary change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_diet" title="Low-carbon diet">Low-carbon diet</a></div>
<p>Overall, food accounts for the largest share of consumption-based GHG emissions with nearly 20% of the global carbon footprint, followed by housing, mobility, services, manufactured products, and construction. Food and services are more significant in poor countries, while mobility and manufactured goods are more significant in rich countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fleurbaey-etal-2014_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-fleurbaey-etal-2014-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>327</span></sup> A 2014 study into the real-life diets of British people estimates their greenhouse gas contributions (<a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>eq</a>) to be: 7.19<span class="nowrap"> </span>kg/day for high meat-eaters through to 3.81<span class="nowrap"> </span>kg/day for vegetarians and 2.89<span class="nowrap"> </span>kg/day for vegans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scarborough-etal-2014_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-scarborough-etal-2014-55">[55]</a></sup>
The widespread adoption of a vegetarian diet could cut food-related greenhouse gas emissions by 63% by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvey-2016_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvey-2016-56">[56]</a></sup>
China introduced new dietary guidelines in 2016 which aim to cut meat consumption by 50% and thereby reduce greenhouse gas emissions by 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion tonnes by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milman-2016_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-milman-2016-57">[57]</a></sup> A 2016 study concluded that taxes on meat and milk could simultaneously result in reduced greenhouse gas emissions and healthier diets. The study analyzed surcharges of 40% on beef and 20% on milk and suggests that an optimum plan would reduce emissions by 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion tonnes per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carrington-2016_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-2016-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-springmann-etal-2016_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-springmann-etal-2016-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_efficiency_and_conservation">Energy efficiency and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">Efficient energy use</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Led-lampa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Led-lampa.jpg/220px-Led-lampa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Led-lampa.jpg/330px-Led-lampa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Led-lampa.jpg/440px-Led-lampa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Led-lampa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 230-volt LED light bulb, with an <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">E27</a> base (10 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a>, 806 <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumens</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>Efficient energy use, sometimes simply called "energy efficiency", is the goal of efforts to reduce the amount of energy required to provide products and services. For example, <a href="/wiki/Building_insulation" title="Building insulation">insulating a home</a> allows a building to use less heating and cooling energy to achieve and maintain a comfortable temperature. Installing <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">LED lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lighting</a>, or natural <a href="/wiki/Skylight" title="Skylight">skylight windows</a> reduces the amount of energy required to attain the same level of illumination compared to using traditional <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulbs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">Compact fluorescent lamps</a> use only 33% of the energy and may last 6 to 10 times longer than incandescent lights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> LED lamps use only about 10% of the energy an incandescent lamp requires.
</p><p>Energy efficiency has proved to be a cost-effective strategy for building economies without necessarily growing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" title="World energy resources and consumption">energy consumption</a>. For example, the state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> began implementing energy-efficiency measures in the mid-1970s, including building code and appliance standards with strict efficiency requirements. During the following years, California's energy consumption has remained approximately flat on a per capita basis while national US consumption doubled. As part of its strategy, California implemented a "loading order" for new energy resources that puts energy efficiency first, renewable electricity supplies second, and new fossil-fired power plants last.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a> is broader than energy efficiency in that it encompasses using less energy to achieve a lesser energy demanding service, for example through behavioral change, as well as encompassing energy efficiency. Examples of conservation without efficiency improvements would be heating a room less in winter, driving less, or working in a less brightly lit room. As with other definitions, the boundary between efficient energy use and energy conservation can be fuzzy, but both are important in environmental and economic terms. This is especially the case when actions are directed at the saving of fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reducing energy use is seen as a key solution to the problem of reducing greenhouse gas emissions. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, improved energy efficiency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy-efficient_buildings" title="Energy-efficient buildings">buildings</a>, industrial processes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable_transportation" title="Sustainable transportation">transportation</a> could reduce the world's energy needs in 2050 by one third, and help control global emissions of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand-side_switching_sources">Demand-side switching sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demand-side switching sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Fuel switching on the demand side refers to changing the type of fuel used to satisfy a need for an energy service. To meet deep decarbonization goals, like the 80% reduction by 2050 goal being discussed in California and the European Union, many primary energy changes are needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Energy efficiency alone may not be sufficient to meet these goals, switching fuels used on the demand side will help lower carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Progressively coal, oil and eventually natural gas for space and water heating in buildings will need to be reduced. For an equivalent amount of heat, burning natural gas produces about 45 per cent less carbon dioxide than burning <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gasdotorg_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-gasdotorg-68">[68]</a></sup> There are various ways in which this could happen, and different strategies will likely make sense in different locations. While the system efficiency of a gas furnace may be higher than the combination of natural gas power plant and electric heat, the combination of the same natural gas power plant and an electric <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">heat pump</a> has lower emissions per unit of heat delivered in all but the coldest climates. This is possible because of the very efficient <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_performance" title="Coefficient of performance">coefficient of performance</a> of heat pumps.
</p><p>At the beginning of this century 70% of all electricity was generated by fossil fuels, and as carbon free sources eventually make up half of the generation mix, replacing gas or oil furnaces and water heaters with electric ones will have a climate benefit. In areas like Norway, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> that have abundant hydroelectricity, electric heat and hot water are common.
</p><p>The economics of switching the demand side from fossil fuels to electricity for heating, will depend on the price of fuels vs electricity and the relative prices of the equipment. The EIA Annual Energy Outlook 2014 suggests that domestic gas prices will rise faster than electricity prices which will encourage electrification in the coming decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
Electrifying heating loads may also provide a flexible resource that can participate in <a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">demand response</a>. Since thermostatically controlled loads have inherent energy storage, electrification of heating could provide a valuable resource to integrate variable renewable resources into the grid.
</p><p>Alternatives to electrification, include decarbonizing pipeline gas through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuels" title="Carbon-neutral fuels">carbon-neutral fuels</a>. A 2015 study by Energy+Environmental Economics shows that a hybrid approach of decarbonizing pipeline gas, electrification, and energy efficiency can meet carbon reduction goals at a similar cost as only electrification and energy efficiency in Southern California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand_side_grid_management">Demand side grid management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Demand side grid management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Expanding intermittent electrical sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, creates a growing problem balancing grid fluctuations. Some of the plans include building pumped storage or continental <a href="/wiki/Super_grid" title="Super grid">super grids</a> costing billions of dollars. However instead of building for more power, there are a variety of ways to affect the size and timing of electricity demand on the consumer side. Designing for reduced demands on a smaller power grid is more efficient and economic than having extra generation and transmission for intermittentcy, power failures and peak demands. Having these abilities is one of the chief aims of a <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">smart grid</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Net_energy_metering#Time_of_use_metering" title="Net energy metering">Time of use</a> metering is a common way to motivate electricity users to reduce their peak load consumption. For instance, running dishwashers and laundry at night after the peak has passed, reduces electricity costs.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_demand" title="Dynamic demand">Dynamic demand</a> plans have devices passively shut off when stress is sensed on the electrical grid. This method may work very well with thermostats, when power on the grid sags a small amount, a low power temperature setting is automatically selected reducing the load on the grid. For instance millions of refrigerators reduce their consumption when clouds pass over solar installations. Consumers would need to have a <a href="/wiki/Smart_meter" title="Smart meter">smart meter</a> in order for the utility to calculate credits.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Demand_response" title="Demand response">Demand response</a> devices could receive all sorts of messages from the grid. The message could be a request to use a low power mode similar to dynamic demand, to shut off entirely during a sudden failure on the grid, or notifications about the current and expected prices for power. This would allow electric cars to recharge at the least expensive rates independent of the time of day. The <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">vehicle-to-grid</a> suggestion would use a car's battery or fuel cell to supply the grid temporarily.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_energy_sources">Alternative energy sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Alternative energy sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy">Renewable energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Renewable energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy commercialization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/330px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/440px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The worldwide growth of renewable energy is shown by the green and dark blue lines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 22,500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a> <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a> <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, the largest hydroelectric power station in the world.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1801" data-file-width="2693" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/220px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/330px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg/440px-Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Shepherds_Flat_Wind_Farm" title="Shepherds Flat Wind Farm">Shepherds Flat Wind Farm</a> is an 845 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW) <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farm</a> in the US state of <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, each turbine is a nameplate 2 or 2.5 MW electricity generator.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/220px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/330px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/440px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 150 MW <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" title="Andasol solar power station">Andasol solar power station</a> is a commercial <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic trough</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_thermal" title="Solar thermal">solar thermal</a> power plant, located in <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Spain" title="Renewable energy in Spain">Spain</a>. The Andasol plant uses tanks of molten salt to store solar energy so that it can continue generating electricity for 7.5 hours after the sun has stopped shining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar-Panel-Cooker-in-front-of-hut.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="322" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Solar-Panel-Cooker-in-front-of-hut.jpg/220px-Solar-Panel-Cooker-in-front-of-hut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Solar-Panel-Cooker-in-front-of-hut.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar-Panel-Cooker-in-front-of-hut.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Solar_cooker" title="Solar cooker">Solar cookers</a> use sunlight as energy source for outdoor cooking.</div></div></div>
<p>Renewable energy flows involve natural phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, rain, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">plant growth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">geothermal heat</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> explains:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Renewable energy is derived from natural processes that are replenished constantly. In its various forms, it derives directly from the sun, or from heat generated deep within the earth. Included in the definition is electricity and heat generated from solar, wind, ocean, <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>, biomass, geothermal resources, and biofuels and hydrogen derived from renewable resources.
</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Climate change concerns</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieapress_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieapress-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP2_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP2-76">[76]</a></sup> and the need to reduce carbon emissions are driving increasing growth in the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_industry" title="Renewable energy industry">renewable energy industries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNnews_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNnews-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mak_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-mak-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-79">[79]</a></sup> Low-carbon renewable energy replaces conventional fossil fuels in three main areas: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generation" title="Power generation">power generation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_hot_water" title="Solar hot water">hot water</a>/ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transport_fuel" title="Transport fuel">transport fuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren15_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren15-80">[80]</a></sup> In 2011, the share of renewables in <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> worldwide grew for the fourth year in a row to 20.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/REN21" title="REN21">REN21</a>'s 2014 report, renewables contributed 19% to supply global energy consumption. This energy consumption is divided as 9% coming from burning biomass, 4.2% as heat energy (non-biomass), 3.8% hydro electricity and 2% as electricity from wind, solar, geothermal, and biomass thermal power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy use</a> has grown much faster than anyone anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pg11_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-pg11-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has said that there are few fundamental technological limits to integrating a portfolio of renewable energy technologies to meet most of total global energy demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2011_17_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2011_17-84">[84]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of energy supply.
</p><p>As of 2012, renewable energy accounts for almost half of new electricity capacity installed and costs are continuing to fall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irena111_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-irena111-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_policy" title="Public policy">Public policy</a> and political leadership helps to "level the playing field" and drive the wider acceptance of renewable energy technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyfocus.com_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyfocus.com-86">[86]</a></sup> As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 118 countries have targets for their own renewable energy futures, and have enacted wide-ranging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_policies" title="Public policies">public policies</a> to promote renewables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-map.ren21.net_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-map.ren21.net-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-88">[88]</a></sup> Leading renewable energy companies include <a href="/wiki/BrightSource_Energy" title="BrightSource Energy">BrightSource Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Solar" title="First Solar">First Solar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamesa_Corporaci%C3%B3n_Tecnol%C3%B3gica" title="Gamesa Corporación Tecnológica">Gamesa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GE_Energy" title="GE Energy">GE Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goldwind" title="Goldwind">Goldwind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sinovel" title="Sinovel">Sinovel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suntech" title="Suntech">Suntech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trina_Solar" title="Trina Solar">Trina Solar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vestas" title="Vestas">Vestas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yingli" title="Yingli">Yingli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-top_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-top-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keith_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-keith-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The incentive to use <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> has been created by <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> and other ecological as well as economic concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pg11_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-pg11-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">Mark Z. Jacobson</a> says producing all new energy with <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> by 2030 is feasible and existing energy supply arrangements could be replaced by 2050. Barriers to implementing the renewable energy plan are seen to be "primarily social and political, not technological or economic". Jacobson says that energy costs with a wind, solar, water system should be similar to today's energy costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enpol2011_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-enpol2011-91">[91]</a></sup> According to a 2011 projection by the (IEA)<a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, solar power generators may produce most of the world's electricity within 50 years, dramatically reducing harmful greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieaiea2011_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ieaiea2011-92">[92]</a></sup> Critics of the "100% renewable energy" approach include <a href="/wiki/Vaclav_Smil" title="Vaclav Smil">Vaclav Smil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_E._Hansen" title="James E. Hansen">James E. Hansen</a>. Smil and Hansen are concerned about the <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable output</a> of solar and wind power, <a href="/wiki/NIMBY" title="NIMBY">NIMBYism</a>, and a lack of infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Economic analysts expect market gains for <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> (and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficient energy use</a>) following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Japanese_nuclear_accidents" title="2011 Japanese nuclear accidents">2011 Japanese nuclear accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In his 2012 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">State of the Union</a> address, President Barack Obama restated his commitment to renewable energy and mentioned the long-standing Interior Department commitment to permit 10,000 MW of renewable energy projects on public land in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindsay_Morris_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindsay_Morris-96">[96]</a></sup> Globally, there are an estimated 3 million direct jobs in renewable energy industries, with about half of them in the biofuels industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren9_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren9-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some countries, with favorable <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> well suited to an economical exploitation of renewable energy sources, already get most of their electricity from renewables, including from <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a> in Iceland (100 percent), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric" title="Hydroelectric">hydroelectric</a> power in Brazil (85 percent), Austria (62 percent), New Zealand (65 percent), and Sweden (54 percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren53_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren53-98">[98]</a></sup> Renewable power generators are spread across many countries, with wind power providing a significant share of electricity in some regional areas: for example, 14 percent in the US state of Iowa, 40 percent in the northern German state of Schleswig-Holstein, and 20 percent in Denmark. <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">Solar water heating</a> makes an important and growing contribution in many countries, most notably in China, which now has 70 percent of the global total (180 GWth). Worldwide, total installed solar water heating systems meet a portion of the water heating needs of over 70 million households. The use of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> for heating continues to grow as well. In Sweden, national use of biomass energy has surpassed that of oil. Direct <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">geothermal heating</a> is also growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren53_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ren53-98">[98]</a></sup> Renewable <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> for transportation, such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>, have contributed to a significant decline in oil consumption in the United States since 2006. The 93 billion liters of biofuels produced worldwide in 2009 displaced the equivalent of an estimated 68 billion liters of gasoline, equal to about 5 percent of world gasoline production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren53_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-ren53-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (September 2018)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Climate_change_mitigation#Dubious" title="Talk:Climate change mitigation">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tnone center"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:458px;max-width:458px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IvanpahRunning.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4912" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/225px-IvanpahRunning.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/338px-IvanpahRunning.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/450px-IvanpahRunning.JPG 2x" width="225"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/225px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/338px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/450px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Some of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> stations: <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah</a> (CSP) and <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz</a> (PV), both in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div></div></div>
