<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power">Nuclear power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nuclear power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3668" data-file-width="2791" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/200px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/300px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg/400px-Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Advanced_Test_Reactor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blue <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">Cherenkov</a> light being produced near the core of the Fission powered <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Test_Reactor" title="Advanced Test Reactor">Advanced Test Reactor</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since about 2001 the term "nuclear renaissance" has been used to refer to a possible <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> industry revival, driven by rising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" title="Price of petroleum">fossil fuel prices</a> and new concerns about meeting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emission limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro-99">[99]</a></sup> However, in March 2011 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a> in Japan and associated shutdowns at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Japanese_nuclear_incidents" title="2011 Japanese nuclear incidents">other nuclear facilities</a> raised questions among some commentators over the future of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platts" title="Platts">Platts</a> has reported that "the crisis at Japan's Fukushima nuclear plants has prompted leading energy-consuming countries to review the safety of their existing reactors and cast doubt on the speed and scale of planned expansions around the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> has reported that nuclear electricity generation in 2012 was at its lowest level since 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna13_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-104">[104]</a></sup> Several previous international studies and assessments,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sims,_R.E.H.,_et_al._105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sims,_R.E.H.,_et_al.-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_2008_42_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA_2008_42-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> suggested that as part of the portfolio of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-carbon_energy" title="Low-carbon energy">low-carbon energy</a> technologies, nuclear power will continue to play a role in reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Historically, nuclear power usage is estimated to have prevented the atmospheric emission of 64 giga<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO2-equivalent</a> as of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Public concerns about nuclear power include the <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">fate of spent nuclear fuel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" title="Nuclear accidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">security risks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">a concern that nuclear power plants are very expensive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bm_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-bm-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Of these concerns, nuclear accidents and disposal of long-lived radioactive fuel/"waste" have probably had the greatest public impact worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bm_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-bm-109">[109]</a></sup> Although generally unaware of it, both of these glaring public concerns are greatly diminished by present <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_safety" title="Passive safety">passive safety</a> designs, the experimentally proven, "melt-down proof" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-II" title="EBR-II">EBR-II</a>, future <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">molten salt reactors</a>, and the use of conventional and more advanced fuel/"waste" <a href="/wiki/Pyroprocessing" title="Pyroprocessing">pyroprocessing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> with the latter recycling or reprocessing not presently being commonplace as it is often considered to be cheaper to use a once-through <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a> in many countries, depending on the varying levels of intrinsic value given by a society in reducing the long-lived waste in their country, with France doing a considerable amount of reprocessing when compared to the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a>, with a 10.6% share of world electricity production as of 2013, is second only to hydroelectricity as the largest source of low-carbon power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Over 400 reactors generate electricity in 31 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> review published in the Journal of Industrial Ecology analyzing CO<sub>2</sub> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">life cycle assessment</a>(LCA) emissions from nuclear power(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a>) determined that: "The collective LCA literature indicates that life cycle <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">GHG</a> emissions from nuclear power are only a fraction of traditional fossil sources and comparable to renewable technologies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE-117">[117]</a></sup>
While some have raised uncertainty surrounding the future GHG emissions of nuclear power as a result of an extreme potential decline in uranium ore grade without a corresponding increase in the <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">efficiency of enrichment methods</a>. In a scenario analysis of future global nuclear development, as it could be effected by a decreasing global <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">uranium market</a> of average ore grade, the analysis determined that depending on conditions, median life cycle nuclear power GHG emissions could be between 9 and 110 g CO<sub>2</sub>-eq/kWh by 2050, with the latter high figure being derived from a "worst-case scenario" that is not "considered very robust" by the authors of the paper, as the "ore grade" in the scenario is lower than the uranium concentration in many lignite coal ashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although this future analyses primarily deals with extrapolations for present <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">Generation II reactor</a> technology, the same paper also summarizes the literature on "FBRs"/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Fast Breeder Reactor">Fast Breeder Reactors</a>, of which two are in operation as of 2014 with the newest being the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800</a>, for these reactors it states that the "median life cycle GHG emissions ... [are] similar to or lower than [present light water reactors] <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LWR" title="LWR">LWRs</a> and purports to consume little or no uranium ore.<i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Warner_+_Heath,_JoIE-117">[117]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>In their 2014 report, the IPCC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparisons_of_life-cycle_greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Comparisons of life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions">comparison of energy sources global warming potential per unit of electricity generated</a>, which notably included <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> effects, mirror the <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> emission value derived from the Warner and Heath Yale <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> for the more common non-breeding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent</a> value of 12 g CO<sub>2</sub>-eq/kWh, which is the lowest global warming forcing of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> power sources, with comparable low carbon power baseload sources, such as hydropower and biomass, producing substantially more global warming forcing 24 and 230 g CO<sub>2</sub>-eq/kWh respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> published <i>The Net Benefits of Low and No-Carbon Electricity Technologies</i> which states, after performing an energy and emissions cost analysis, that "The net benefits of new nuclear, hydro, and natural gas combined cycle plants far outweigh the net benefits of new wind or solar plants", with the most cost effective low carbon power technology being determined to be nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_United_States" title="President of United States">presidential</a> campaign, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> stated, "Nuclear power represents more than 70% of our noncarbon generated electricity. It is unlikely that we can meet our aggressive climate goals if we eliminate nuclear power as an option."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="758" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/220px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/330px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg/440px-Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Net_US_Electric_Generation_for_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This graph illustrates nuclear power is the United States's largest contributor of non-greenhouse-gas-emitting electric power generation, comprising nearly three-quarters of the non-emitting sources.</div></div></div>
<p>Analysis in 2015 by Professor and Chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation's building programs in the 1980s, within 10 years nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synfuels" title="Synfuels">synfuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation's already proven decadal rates(in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a>(GW/year/$).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>This is in contrast to the completely conceptual paper-studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude" title="Orders of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> more costly global investment per year, an investment rate that has no historical precedent, having never been attempted due to its prohibitive cost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and with far greater land area that would be required to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, along with the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qualenergia.it_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-qualenergia.it-126">[126]</a></sup> As Brook notes the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power may be uncompetitive compared with fossil fuel energy sources in countries without a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> program, and in comparison to a fossil fuel plant of the same power output, nuclear power plants take a longer amount of time to construct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tf2010_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-tf2010-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea2009_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-iea2009-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two new, first of their kind, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor" title="European Pressurized Reactor">EPR</a> reactors under construction in Finland and France have been delayed and are running over-budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jk_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-jk-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeninc_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rb_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-rb-134">[134]</a></sup> However learning from experience, two further EPR reactors under construction in China are on, and ahead, of schedule respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> As of 2013, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Nuclear_Society" title="European Nuclear Society">European Nuclear Society</a>, worldwide there were 68 civil nuclear power reactors under construction in 15 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euronuclear.org_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-euronuclear.org-137">[137]</a></sup> China has 29 of these nuclear power reactors under construction, as of 2013, with plans to build many more,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euronuclear.org_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-euronuclear.org-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_China_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_China-138">[138]</a></sup> while in the US the licenses of almost half its reactors have been extended to 60 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA-139">[139]</a></sup> and plans to build another dozen are under serious consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-matt2010_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-matt2010-140">[140]</a></sup> There are also a considerable number of new reactors being built in South Korea, India, and Russia. At least 100 older and smaller reactors will "most probably be closed over the next 10–15 years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ditt_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-ditt-141">[141]</a></sup> This is probable only if one does not factor in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Light_Water_Reactor_Sustainability_Program" title="Light Water Reactor Sustainability Program">Light Water Reactor Sustainability Program</a>, created to permit the extension of the life span of the USA's 104 nuclear reactors to 60 years. The licenses of almost half of the USA's reactors have been extended to 60 years as of 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_the_USA-139">[139]</a></sup> Two new "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_safety" title="Passive safety">passive safety</a>" <a href="/wiki/AP1000" title="AP1000">AP1000</a> reactors are, as of 2013, being constructed at <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a>.
</p><p>Public opinion about nuclear power varies widely between countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> A poll by Gallup International (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> assessed public opinion in 47 countries. The poll was conducted following a <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">tsunami and earthquake</a> which caused an <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">accident</a> at the Fukushima nuclear power plant in Japan. 49% stated that they held favourable views about nuclear energy, while 43% held an unfavourable view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Another global survey by Ipsos (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> assessed public opinion in 24 countries. Respondents to this survey showed a clear preference for renewable energy sources over coal and nuclear energy (refer to graph opposite).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipsos_2011_preference_for_renewables-2">[2]</a></sup> Ipsos (2012)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> found that solar and wind were viewed by the public as being more environmentally friendly and more viable long-term energy sources relative to nuclear power and natural gas. However, solar and wind were viewed as being less reliable relative to nuclear power and natural gas. In 2012 a poll done in the UK found that 63% of those surveyed support nuclear power, and with opposition to nuclear power at 11%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In Germany, strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear" title="Anti-nuclear">anti-nuclear</a> sentiment led to eight of the seventeen operating reactors being permanently shut down following the March 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annika_Breidthardt_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Annika_Breidthardt-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> research, in the form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor</a> is underway. Fusion powered electricity generation was initially believed to be readily achievable, as fission power had been. However, the extreme requirements for continuous reactions and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_containment" title="Plasma containment">plasma containment</a> led to projections being extended by several decades. In 2010, more than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial power production was still believed to be unlikely before 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-150">[150]</a></sup> Although rather than an either, or, issue economical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion-fission_hybrid_reactor" title="Fusion-fission hybrid reactor">fusion-fission hybrid reactors</a> could be built before any attempt at this more demanding commercial "<a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">pure-fusion reactor</a>"/<a href="/wiki/DEMOnstration_Power_Station" title="DEMOnstration Power Station">DEMO</a> reactor takes place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_to_gas_fuel_switching">Coal to gas fuel switching</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Coal to gas fuel switching">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">Emission intensity</a></div>
<p>Most mitigation proposals imply—rather than directly state—an eventual reduction in global fossil fuel production. Also proposed are direct quotas on global fossil fuel production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas emits far fewer greenhouse gases (i.e. CO<sub>2</sub> and methane—CH<sub>4</sub>) than coal when burned at power plants, but evidence has been emerging that this benefit could be completely negated by methane leakage at gas drilling fields and other points in the supply chain.
</p><p>A study performed by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) and the Gas Research Institute (GRI) in 1997 sought to discover whether the reduction in carbon dioxide emissions from increased natural gas (predominantly methane) use would be offset by a possible increased level of <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> from sources such as leaks and emissions. The study concluded that the reduction in emissions from increased natural gas use outweighs the detrimental effects of increased methane emissions. More recent peer-reviewed studies have challenged the findings of this study, with researchers from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) reconfirming findings of high rates of methane (CH4) leakage from natural gas fields.
</p><p>A 2011 study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usclimatenetwork.org_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-usclimatenetwork.org-154">[154]</a></sup> by noted climate research scientist, Tom Wigley,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> found that while carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>) emissions from fossil fuel combustion may be reduced by using natural gas rather than coal to produce energy, it also found that additional methane (CH4) from leakage adds to the radiative forcing of the climate system, offsetting the reduction in CO<sub>2</sub> forcing that accompanies the transition from coal to gas. The study looked at methane leakage from coal mining; changes in radiative forcing due to changes in the emissions of sulfur dioxide and carbonaceous aerosols; and differences in the efficiency of electricity production between coal- and gas-fired power generation. On balance, these factors more than offset the reduction in warming due to reduced CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. When gas replaces coal there is additional warming out to 2,050 with an assumed leakage rate of 0%, and out to 2,140 if the leakage rate is as high as 10%. The overall effects on global-mean temperature over the 21st century, however, are small. Petron et al. (2013)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-156">[156]</a></sup> and Alvarez et al. (2012)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> note that estimated that leakage from gas infrastructure is likely to be underestimated. These studies indicate that the exploitation of natural gas as a "cleaner" fuel is questionable. A 2014 meta-study of 20 years of natural gas technical literature shows that methane emissions are consistently underestimated but on a 100-year scale, the climate benefits of coal to gas fuel switching are likely larger than the negative effects of natural gas leakage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_pump">Heat pump</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Heat pump">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">Heat pump</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/220px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/330px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg/440px-Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outunit_of_heat_pump.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Outside unit of an air-source heat pump</div></div></div>
<p>A heat pump is a device that provides heat energy from a source of heat to a destination called a "heat sink". Heat pumps are designed to move <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> opposite to the direction of spontaneous heat flow by absorbing heat from a cold space and releasing it to a warmer one. A heat pump uses some amount of external power to accomplish the work of transferring energy from the heat source to the heat sink.
</p><p>While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_conditioner" title="Air conditioner">air conditioners</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freezer" title="Freezer">freezers</a> are familiar examples of heat pumps, the term "heat pump" is more general and applies to many <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">HVAC</a> (heating, ventilating, and air conditioning) devices used for space heating or space cooling. When a heat pump is used for heating, it employs the same basic <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump_and_refrigeration_cycle" title="Heat pump and refrigeration cycle">refrigeration-type cycle</a> used by an air conditioner or a refrigerator, but in the opposite direction—releasing heat into the conditioned space rather than the surrounding environment. In this use, heat pumps generally draw heat from the cooler external air or from the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In heating mode, heat pumps are three to four times <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">more efficient</a> in their use of electric power than simple electrical resistance heaters.
</p><p>It has been concluded that heat pumps are the single technology that could reduce the greenhouse gas emissions of households better than every other technology that is available on the market. With a market share of 30% and (potentially) clean electricity, heat pumps could reduce global CO<sub>2</sub> emissions by 8% annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Using ground source heat pumps could reduce around 60% of the <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> demand and 90% of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions in Europe in 2050 and make handling high shares of renewable energy easier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Using surplus renewable energy in heat pumps is regarded as the most effective household means to reduce global warming and fossil fuel depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>With significant amounts of fossil fuel used in electricity production, demands on the electrical grid also generate greenhouse gases. Without a high share of low-carbon electricity, a domestic heat pump will produce more carbon emissions than using natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuel_phase-out:_carbon_neutral_and_negative_fuels">Fossil fuel phase-out: carbon neutral and negative fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fossil fuel phase-out: carbon neutral and negative fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/220px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/330px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg/440px-Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baggerbesetzung_Welzow_S%C3%BCd.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>3,500–4,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_activist" title="Environmental activist">environmental activists</a> blocking a coal mine in Germany to limit climate change (<a href="/wiki/Ende_Gel%C3%A4nde_2016" title="Ende Gelände 2016">Ende Gelände 2016</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">Fossil fuel phase-out</a></div>
<p>Fossil fuel may be phased-out with <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral and carbon-negative</a> pipeline and transportation fuels created with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a> technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> from fossil fuel <a href="/wiki/Flue_gas" title="Flue gas">flue gas</a> can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Lumber#Plastic_lumber" title="Lumber">plastic lumber</a> allowing carbon negative <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(May 2015)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sinks_and_negative_emissions">Sinks and negative emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sinks and negative emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">Carbon sink</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">Negative carbon dioxide emission</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sink</a> is a natural or artificial reservoir that accumulates and stores some carbon-containing chemical compound for an indefinite period, such as a growing <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">negative carbon dioxide emission</a> on the other hand is a permanent removal of carbon dioxide out of the atmosphere. Examples are <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">direct air capture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_weathering" title="Enhanced weathering">enhanced weathering</a> technologies such as storing it in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_sequestration_of_CO2" title="Geologic sequestration of CO2">geologic formations</a> underground and <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>. These processes are sometimes considered as variations of sinks or mitigation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Technology_Roadmap_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_2009_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Technology_Roadmap_Carbon_Capture_and_Storage_2009-171">[171]</a></sup> and sometimes as geoengineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> In combination with other mitigation measures, sinks in combination with negative carbon emissions are considered crucial for meeting the <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation_scenarios#350_ppm" title="Climate change mitigation scenarios">350 ppm target</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hare_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-hare-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Azar_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Azar-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Antarctic Climate and Ecosystems Cooperative Research Centre (ACE-CRC) notes that one third of humankind's annual emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> are absorbed by the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> However, this also leads to <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, with potentially significant impacts on marine life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Acidification lowers the level of carbonate ions available for calcifying organisms to form their shells. These organisms include plankton species that contribute to the foundation of the Southern Ocean food web. However acidification may impact on a broad range of other physiological and ecological processes, such as fish respiration, larval development and changes in the solubility of both nutrients and toxins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforestation_and_afforestation">Reforestation and afforestation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reforestation and afforestation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1490" data-file-width="2234" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/220px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/330px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg/440px-Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shennongjia_virgin_forest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transferring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_rights" title="Land rights">land rights</a> to indigenous inhabitants is argued to efficiently conserve forests.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">Reforestation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosequestration" title="Biosequestration">Biosequestration</a></div>
<p>Almost 20 percent (8<span class="nowrap"> </span>GtCO<sub>2</sub>/year) of total greenhouse-gas emissions were from deforestation in 2007. It is estimated that avoided deforestation reduces CO<sub>2</sub> emissions at a rate of 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>tonne of CO<sub>2</sub> per $1–5 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opportunity_costs" title="Opportunity costs">opportunity costs</a> from lost agriculture. <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">Reforestation</a> could save at least another 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>GtCO<sub>2</sub>/year, at an estimated cost of $5–15/tCO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern-179">[179]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Afforestation" title="Afforestation">Afforestation</a> is where there was previously no forest - such plantations are estimated to have to be prohibitively massive to be reduce emissions by itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Transferring rights over land from public domain to its indigenous inhabitants, who have had a stake for millennia in preserving the forests that they depend on, is argued to be a cost effective strategy to conserve forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian1_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-181">[181]</a></sup> This includes the protection of such rights entitled in existing laws, such as India's <a href="/wiki/The_Scheduled_Tribes_and_Other_Traditional_Forest_Dwellers_(Recognition_of_Forest_Rights)_Act,_2006" title="The Scheduled Tribes and Other Traditional Forest Dwellers (Recognition of Forest Rights) Act, 2006">Forest Rights Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian1_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian1-181">[181]</a></sup> The transferring of such rights in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, perhaps the largest <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> in modern times, has been argued to have increased forest cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fp0718_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-fp0718-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rightsandresources_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-rightsandresources-183">[183]</a></sup> Granting title of the land has shown to have two or three times less clearing than even state run parks, notably in the Brazilian Amazon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ding-etal-2016-webpage_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-ding-etal-2016-webpage-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ding-etal-2016_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-ding-etal-2016-185">[185]</a></sup> Excluding humans and even evicting inhabitants from protected areas (called "fortress conservation"), sometimes as a result of lobbying by environmental groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> often lead to more exploitation of the land as the native inhabitants then turn to work for extractive companies to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fp0718_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-fp0718-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>With increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" title="Intensive agriculture">intensive agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, there is an increase in the amount of abandoned farmland. By some estimates, for every half a hectare of original <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old-growth forest</a> cut down, more than 20 hectares of new <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">secondary forests</a> are growing, even though they do not have the same biodiversity as the original forests and original forests store 60% more carbon than these new secondary forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> According to a study in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, promoting regrowth on abandoned farmland could offset years of carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avoided_desertification">Avoided desertification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Avoided desertification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2190" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/220px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/330px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cow_female_black_white.jpg/440px-Cow_female_black_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cow_female_black_white.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Managed grazing methods are argued to be able to restore grasslands, thereby significantly decreasing atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASavory_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASavory-190">[190]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Restoring grasslands store CO<sub>2</sub> from the air into plant material. Grazing livestock, usually not left to wander, would eat the grass and would minimize any grass growth. However, grass left alone would eventually grow to cover its own growing buds, preventing them from photosynthesizing and the dying plant would stay in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tlovell_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-191">[191]</a></sup> A method proposed to restore grasslands uses fences with many small paddocks and moving herds from one paddock to another after a day a two in order to mimick natural grazers and allowing the grass to grow optimally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tlovell_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Additionally, when part of leaf matter is consumed by a herding animal, a corresponding amount of root matter is sloughed off too as it would not be able to sustain the previous amount of root matter and while most of the lost root matter would rot and enter the atmosphere, part of the carbon is sequestered into the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tlovell_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-191">[191]</a></sup> It is estimated that increasing the carbon content of the soils in the world's 3.5 billion hectares of agricultural grassland by 1% would offset nearly 12 years of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tlovell_191-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tlovell-191">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Savory" title="Allan Savory">Allan Savory</a>, as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic_management" title="Holistic management">holistic management</a>, claims that while large herds are often blamed for <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, prehistoric lands supported large or larger herds and areas where herds were removed in the United States are still desertifying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASavory_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-ASavory-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, the global warming induced thawing of the <a href="/wiki/Permafrost#Climate_change_effects" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, which stores about two times the amount of the carbon currently released in the atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> releases the potent greenhouse gas, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">methane</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback cycle</a> that is feared to lead to a <a href="/wiki/Tipping_point_(climatology)" title="Tipping point (climatology)">tipping point</a> called <a href="/wiki/Runaway_climate_change" title="Runaway climate change">runaway climate change</a>. A method proposed to prevent such a scenario is to bring back large herbivores such as seen in <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_Park" title="Pleistocene Park">Pleistocene Park</a>, where their trampling naturally keep the ground cooler by eliminating shrubs and keeping the ground exposed to the cold air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_capture_and_storage">Carbon capture and storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Carbon capture and storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="530" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/350px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/525px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg/700px-Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_sequestration-2009-10-07.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic showing both terrestrial and geological sequestration of carbon dioxide emissions from a coal-fired plant</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">Carbon capture and storage</a></div>
<p>Carbon capture and storage (CCS) is a method to mitigate climate change by capturing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) from large point sources such as power plants and subsequently storing it away safely instead of releasing it into the atmosphere. The IPCC estimates that the costs of halting global warming would double without CCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian0916_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian0916-196">[196]</a></sup> The International Energy Agency says CCS is "the most important single new technology for CO<sub>2</sub> savings" in power generation and industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-197">[197]</a></sup> Though it requires up to 40% more energy to run a CCS coal power plant than a regular coal plant, CCS could potentially capture about 90% of all the carbon emitted by the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-197">[197]</a></sup> Norway's <a href="/wiki/Sleipner_gas_field" title="Sleipner gas field">Sleipner gas field</a>, beginning in 1996, stores almost a million tons of CO<sub>2</sub> a year to avoid penalties in producing natural gas with unusually high levels of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futureenergysummit_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-futureenergysummit-197">[197]</a></sup> As of late 2011, the total planned CO<sub>2</sub> storage capacity of all 14 projects in operation or under construction is over 33 million tonnes a year. This is broadly equivalent to preventing the emissions from more than six million cars from entering the atmosphere each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Club" title="Sierra Club">Sierra Club</a> analysis, the US coal fired <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper Project</a> due to be online in 2017, is the most expensive power plant ever built for the watts of electricity it will generate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drajem_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drajem-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_weathering">Enhanced weathering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Enhanced weathering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Enhanced weathering is the removal of carbon from the air into the earth, enhancing the natural carbon cycle where carbon is mineralized into rock. The <a href="/wiki/CarbFix" title="CarbFix">CarbFix</a> project couples with carbon capture and storage in power plants to turn carbon dioxide into stone in a relatively short period of two years, addressing the common concern of leakage in CCS projects. While this project used <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> rocks, <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> has also shown promise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian0916_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian0916-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geoengineering">Geoengineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Geoengineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">Climate engineering</a></div>
<p>Geoengineering is seen by Olivier Sterck as an alternative to mitigation and adaptation, but by Gernot Wagner as an entirely separate response to climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> In a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">literature</a> assessment, Barker <i>et al.</i> (2007) described geoengineering as a type of mitigation policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> IPCC (2007) concluded that geoengineering options, such as ocean fertilization to remove CO<sub>2</sub> from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, remained largely unproven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> It was judged that reliable cost estimates for geoengineering had not yet been published.
</p><p>Chapter 28 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">National Academy of Sciences</a> report <i>Policy Implications of Greenhouse Warming: Mitigation, Adaptation, and the Science Base</i> (1992) defined geoengineering as "options that would involve large-scale engineering of our environment in order to combat or counteract the effects of changes in atmospheric chemistry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nas_policy_implications_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-nas_policy_implications-205">[205]</a></sup> They evaluated a range of options to try to give preliminary answers to two questions: can these options work and could they be carried out with a reasonable cost. They also sought to encourage discussion of a third question — what adverse side effects might there be. The following types of option were examined: reforestation, increasing ocean absorption of carbon dioxide (carbon sequestration) and screening out some sunlight. NAS also argued "Engineered countermeasures need to be evaluated but should not be implemented without broad understanding of the direct effects and the potential side effects, the ethical issues, and the risks.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nas_policy_implications_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-nas_policy_implications-205">[205]</a></sup> In July 2011 a report by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Government_Accountability_Office" title="United States Government Accountability Office">United States Government Accountability Office</a> on geoengineering found that "[c]limate engineering technologies do not now offer a viable response to global climate change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_removal">Carbon dioxide removal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide removal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">Carbon dioxide removal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_remediation" title="Greenhouse gas remediation">Greenhouse gas remediation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">Carbon sequestration</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_air_capture" title="Carbon air capture">Carbon air capture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_removal" title="Carbon dioxide removal">Carbon dioxide removal</a> has been proposed as a method of reducing the amount of radiative forcing. A variety of means of artificially capturing and storing carbon, as well as of enhancing natural sequestration processes, are being explored. The main natural process is <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by plants and single-celled organisms (see <a href="/wiki/Biosequestration" title="Biosequestration">biosequestration</a>). Artificial processes vary, and concerns have been expressed about the long-term effects of some of these processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is notable that the availability of cheap energy and appropriate sites for <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">geological storage</a> of carbon may make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_air_capture" title="Carbon dioxide air capture">carbon dioxide air capture</a> viable commercially. It is, however, generally expected that carbon dioxide air capture may be uneconomic when compared to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> from major sources — in particular, fossil fuel powered power stations, refineries, etc. As in the case of the US <a href="/wiki/Kemper_Project" title="Kemper Project">Kemper Project</a> with carbon capture, costs of energy produced will grow significantly. However, captured CO<sub>2</sub> can be used to force more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statoil" title="Statoil">Statoil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell</a> have made plans to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> can also be used in commercial <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a>, giving an opportunity to kick-start the technology. Some attempts have been made to use <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> to capture <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smokestack" title="Smokestack">smokestack</a> emissions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> notably the <a href="/wiki/GreenFuel_Technologies_Corporation" title="GreenFuel Technologies Corporation">GreenFuel Technologies Corporation</a>, who have now shut down operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_radiation_management">Solar radiation management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Solar radiation management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_radiation_management" title="Solar radiation management">Solar radiation management</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stratospheric_aerosol_injection_(climate_engineering)" title="Stratospheric aerosol injection (climate engineering)">Stratospheric aerosol injection (climate engineering)</a></div>
<p>The main purpose of solar radiation management seek to reflect sunlight and thus reduce global warming. The ability of stratospheric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfate_aerosols" title="Sulfate aerosols">sulfate aerosols</a> to create a <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">global dimming</a> effect has made them a possible candidate for use in <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-CO2_greenhouse_gases">Non-CO<sub>2</sub> greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Non-CO2 greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CO<sub>2</sub> is not the only GHG relevant to mitigation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> and governments have acted to regulate the emissions of other GHGs emitted by human activities (<a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> GHGs). The emissions caps agreed to by most developed countries under the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> regulate the emissions of almost all the anthropogenic GHGs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>
These gases are CO<sub>2</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O), the <a href="/wiki/Halocarbon" title="Halocarbon">hydrofluorocarbons</a> (HFC), perfluorocarbons (PFC), and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> (SF<sub>6</sub>).
</p><p>Stabilizing the atmospheric concentrations of the different anthropogenic GHGs requires an understanding of their different physical properties. Stabilization depends both on how quickly GHGs are added to the atmosphere and how fast they are removed. The rate of removal is measured by the atmospheric lifetime of the GHG in question (see the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Global_warming_potential" title="Greenhouse gas">main GHG article</a> for a list). Here, the lifetime is defined as the time required for a given perturbation of the GHG in the atmosphere to be reduced to 37% of its initial amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup>
Methane has a relatively short atmospheric lifetime of about 12 years, while N<sub>2</sub>O's lifetime is about 110 years. For methane, a reduction of about 30% below current emission levels would lead to a stabilization in its atmospheric concentration, while for N<sub>2</sub>O, an emissions reduction of more than 50% would be required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-meehl_stabilizing_ghg_concentrations-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> is a significantly more potent greenhouse gas than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> in the amount of heat it can trap, especially in the short term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> Burning one molecule of methane generates one molecule of carbon dioxide, indicating there may be no net benefit in using gas as a fuel source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usclimatenetwork.org_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-usclimatenetwork.org-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-156">[156]</a></sup> Reducing the amount of waste methane produced in the first place and moving away from use of gas as a fuel source will have a greater beneficial impact, as might other approaches to productive use of otherwise-wasted methane. In terms of prevention, vaccines are being developed in Australia to reduce the significant global warming contributions from <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> released by livestock via <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eructation" title="Eructation">eructation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another physical property of the anthropogenic GHGs relevant to mitigation is the different abilities of the gases to trap heat (in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>). Some gases are more effective at trapping heat than others, e.g., SF<sub>6</sub> is 22,200 times more effective a GHG than CO<sub>2</sub> on a per-kilogram basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
A measure for this physical property is the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (GWP), and is used in the Kyoto Protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although not designed for this purpose, the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> has probably benefited climate change mitigation efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup>
The Montreal Protocol is an international <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> that has successfully reduced emissions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone-depleting_substance" title="Ozone-depleting substance">ozone-depleting substances</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>), which are also greenhouse gases.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_sector">By sector</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: By sector">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TeslaRoadster-front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/220px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/330px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/TeslaRoadster-front.jpg/440px-TeslaRoadster-front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TeslaRoadster-front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Roadster_(2008)" title="Tesla Roadster (2008)">Tesla Roadster (2008)</a> emits no tailpipe emissions, uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li-Ion" title="Li-Ion">lithium ion</a> batteries to achieve 220 mi (350 km) per charge, while also capable of going 0–60 in under 4 seconds.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="720" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/220px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/330px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg/440px-BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BikesInAmsterdam_2004_SeanMcClean.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">Bicycles</a> have almost no <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> compared to cars, and canal transport may represent a positive option for certain types of freight in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_transport" title="Sustainable transport">Sustainable transport</a></div>
<p>Transportation emissions account for roughly 1/4 of emissions worldwide, and are even more important in terms of impact in developed nations especially in North America and Australia. Many citizens of countries like the United States and Canada who drive personal cars often, see well over half of their climate change impact stemming from the emissions produced from their cars. Modes of mass transportation such as bus, light rail (metro, subway, etc.), and long-distance rail are far and away the most energy-efficient means of motorized transportation for passengers, able to use in many cases over twenty times less energy per person-distance than a personal automobile. Modern <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy-efficient technologies</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> electric vehicles and <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral synthetic gasoline &amp; Jet fuel</a> may also help to reduce the consumption of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, land use changes and emissions of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Utilizing <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail transport</a>, especially electric rail, over the far less efficient <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_aviation" title="Environmental impact of aviation">air transport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Road_transport" title="Road transport">truck transport</a>
significantly reduces emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> With the use of electric trains and cars in transportation there is the opportunity to run them with <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_power" title="Low-carbon power">low-carbon power</a>, producing far fewer emissions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_planning">Urban planning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Urban planning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">Urban planning</a></div>
<p>Effective <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">sprawl</a> aims to decrease Vehicle Miles Travelled (VMT), lowering emissions from transportation. Personal cars are extremely inefficient at moving passengers, while <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transport</a> and bicycles are many times more efficient (as is the simplest form of human transportation, walking). All of these are encouraged by urban/community planning and are an effective way to reduce greenhouse gas emissions. Between 1982 and 1997, the amount of land consumed for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_development" title="Urban development">urban development</a> in the United States increased by 47 percent while the nation's population grew by only 17 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>
Inefficient <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> development practices have increased infrastructure costs as well as the amount of energy needed for transportation, community services, and buildings.
</p><p>At the same time, a growing number of citizens and government officials have begun advocating a smarter approach to land use planning. These <a href="/wiki/Smart_growth" title="Smart growth">smart growth</a> practices include compact community development, multiple transportation choices, mixed land uses, and practices to conserve green space. These programs offer environmental, economic, and quality-of-life benefits; and they also serve to reduce energy usage and greenhouse gas emissions.
</p><p>Approaches such as <a href="/wiki/New_Urbanism" title="New Urbanism">New Urbanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transit-oriented_development" title="Transit-oriented development">transit-oriented development</a> seek to reduce distances travelled, especially by private vehicles, encourage <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transit" title="Public transit">public transit</a> and make <a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">walking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cycling" title="Cycling">cycling</a> more attractive options. This is achieved through "medium-density", <a href="/wiki/Mixed-use_development" title="Mixed-use development">mixed-use planning</a> and the concentration of housing within walking distance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_center" title="Town center">town centers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transport_node" title="Transport node">transport nodes</a>.
</p><p>Smarter growth land use policies have both a direct and indirect effect on energy consuming behavior. For example, transportation energy usage, the number one user of petroleum fuels, could be significantly reduced through more compact and mixed use land development patterns, which in turn could be served by a greater variety of non-automotive based transportation choices.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_design">Building design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_mitigation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Building design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">Sustainable architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">Green building</a></div>
<p>Emissions from <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">housing</a> are substantial,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> and government-supported energy efficiency programmes can make a difference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>For institutions of higher learning in the United States, greenhouse gas emissions depend primarily on total area of buildings and secondarily on climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> If climate is not taken into account, annual greenhouse gas emissions due to energy consumed on campuses plus purchased electricity can be estimated with the formula, <i>E=aS</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where <i>a</i> =0.001621 metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent/square foot or 0.0241 metric tonnes of CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent/square meter and <i>b</i>= 1.1354.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>New buildings can be constructed using <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar_building_design" title="Passive solar building design">passive solar building design</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-energy_building" title="Low-energy building">low-energy building</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Zero-energy_building" title="Zero-energy building">zero-energy building</a> techniques, using <a href="/wiki/Renewable_heat" title="Renewable heat">renewable heat</a> sources.
